    <a name="239"></a>
    <p>INTRODUCTION</p>
    <p>This outline or sketch of Cherokee grammar is intended primarily
      as an aid to the user of the Cherokee-English dictionary.<sup><a
          href="#footnote1">1</a></sup> The authors hope that Cherokee
      speakers who utilize the dictionary will acquire a deeper
      understanding of the complex structure of their language by
      reading the grammar sketch. Students of Cherokee attempting to
      learn the language and linguists interested in studying the
      structure of Cherokee should also find the dictionary and grammar
      outline useful.</p>
    <p>The traditional parts of speech, used to classify the Cherokee
      entries in the dictionary, provide a useful framework by means of
      which the non-linguist can understand the grammatical systems of
      Cherokee. Each of the eight part of speech categories is described
      in the grammar outline. The first section is devoted to a
      discussion of the Cherokee verb. Since the structure of verbs in
      Cherokee is quite complex, this is the longest and most detailed
      section of the grammar sketch. The structure of Cherokee nouns is
      described in the second section, and the remaining parts of speech
      are discussed in the third section. The fourth section of the
      grammar outline consists of notes on various aspects of Cherokee
      syntax, the principles of sentence formation in the language .</p>
    <p>Throughout the grammar sketch examples are provided for each of
      the points discussed. In addition to the examples, the first
      section contains a number of complete and partial verb paradigms.
      In the examples and paradigms we have chosen to spell final
      syllables in their entirety, and the reader should remember that
      final vowels are ordinarily not pronounced in spoken Cherokee.
      Further study of the language is needed to determine the precise
      nature of the rules which govern the dropping of vowels in various
      contexts.<sup><a href="#footnote2">2</a></sup></p>
    <p>Those examples in the grammar outline which can stand alone as
      sentences are followed by periods and are translated by sentences
      in English. This practice is not followed in the case of examples
      which cannot stand alone as sentences. In addition to the
      discussion of the various grammar points and the examples which
      illustrate them, the reader is urged to study the illustrative
      sentences in the dictionary. These sentences provide further
      insight into the structure of the Cherokee language.</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 240</p>
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">[blank]</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 241</p>
    <a name="241"></a>
    <p align="center">Section I. The Verb</p>
    <p>&nbsp;</p>
    <p>Verb forms in Cherokee must contain a verb stem, one or more
      pronoun prefixes, and a final suffix. They may also exhibit one or
      more initial prefixes, a reflexive prefix which directly precedes
      the stem, and one or more non-final suffixes. These facts are
      displayed in Figure 1, with optional elements indicated by ± and
      obligatory elements indicated by +.</p>
    <table align="center" border="1">
      <caption>Figure 1</caption> <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>± initial prefix(es)</td>
          <td>+ pronoun prefix(es)</td>
          <td>± reflexive prefix</td>
          <td>+ verb stem</td>
          <td>± non-final suffix(es)</td>
          <td>+ final suffix</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>I-A.<a name="I-A"></a> <u>The initial prefixes</u>. A verb form
      in Cherokee may optionally exhibit one or more initial prefixes;
      because of their position immediately preceding the pronoun
      prefixes, the initial prefixes are also referred to as
      pre-pronominal prefixes. Since several initial prefixes may occur
      together in a single verb form, it is necessary to describe the
      order in which they appear relative to each other; this
      information is presented in Figure 2</p>
    <table align="center" border="1">
      <caption>Figure 2</caption> <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">1</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">
            <p>y-, negative</p>
            <p>j-, relative,</p>
            <p>past, etc.</p>
          </td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">2</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">w-, away from speaker</td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">3</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">
            <p>n-, lateral position,</p>
            <p>already,</p>
            <p>etc.</p>
          </td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">4</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">de-, plural object</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">5</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">
            <p>da-, future</p>
            <p>da-, motion toward speaker</p>
            <p>di-, distant position</p>
          </td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">6</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">i-, again</td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">7</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">
            <p>ga-, since</p>
            <p>e-, distant imperative</p>
          </td>
          <td style="border-right: medium none;" valign="top">8</td>
          <td style="border-left: medium none;">pronoun prefix(es)</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>Prefixes which are shown in the same box in Figure 2 do not occur
      together; for example, the prefixes y- and ji-, which appear in
      the first box in Figure 2, do not co-occur. In addition, in some
      cases prefixes which occur in different positions cannot occur
      together. This is generally due to an inherent contradiction in
      the meaning of the two prefixes; for example, the w- prefix, which
      indicates that the person spoken of has his back turned to the
      speaker, obviously cannot occur together with the n- prefix which
      indicates that the person spoken of is positioned laterally in
      relation to the speaker. Similarly, the j- prefix which is used
      with certain past tense verb forms cannot co-occur with da- the
      future tense prefix. A discussion of each of the initial prefixes
      follows.</p>
    <p><u>y-, negative, conditional</u></p>
    <p>form : y- before a vowel , yi- before a consonant, yu- before w-;
      cf. (1-3).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(1 )</td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>a¹gowh²tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"He sees it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮭ ᏯᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>hla³ yạ²gowh³tị²ha.</td>
          <td>"He doesn't see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(2)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>jị²gowh¹ti³ha.</td>
          <td>"I see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮭ ᏱᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>hla³ yị²jị²gowh³²tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"I don't see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td colspan="5">
            <hr color="darkgray">
            <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 242</p>
          </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(3)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏩᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>wạ²gowh³tị²ha.</td>
          <td>"He sees it (with his back turned)." </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮭ ᏳᏩᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>hla³ yụ²wạ²gowh³tị²ha.</td>
          <td>"He doesn't see it (with his back turned)." </td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>When y- directly precedes h- , metathesis occurs as in (4).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(4 )</td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>hị²gowh²tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"You see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">Ꮭ ᏱᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>hla³ hyị²gowh³tị²ha.</td>
          <td>"You don't see it."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>function: When y- is used together with the negative word hla
      (tla in some dialects) "not," it serves to negate verb forms, as
      in (1-2). When y- occurs in a verb form not preceded by hla, its
      presence shows uncertainty on the part of the speaker toward the
      occurrence of the action or state expressed by the verb, as in
      (5).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(5) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
          <td>"He is speaking."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏱᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>yị²gạ²wo³²ni⁴ha.</td>
          <td>"if he is speaking"</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>Conditional forms like (5b) <u>must occur as subordinate clauses
        in longer sentences</u>, as in (6).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(6) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏱᎦᏬᏂᎭ, Ꮭ ᏯᏆᏅᏔ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>yị²gạ²wo³²ni⁴ha, hla³ yạ²gwanh³ta.</td>
          <td>"If he is speaking, I don't know it."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>When used to indicate conditionality in past time, y- requires
      the non-progressive form of the verb stem and the suffix
      ordinarily employed with present progressive forms, -a; see (7).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(7)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏳᏬᏂᏌ <br>
          </td>
          <td>yu²wo³²ni⁴sa</td>
          <td>"if he spoke"</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: The addition of y- to verb forms results in pitch
      changes in the verb; the same occurs in the case of other initial
      prefixes also. The rules which determine these changes are not
      fully understood at present.</p>
    <p><u>j- , relative</u></p>
    <p>form: j- before a vowel, ji- before a consonant, cf. (8-9); ju-
      before w-.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(8) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎤᏬᏝ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya u¹wo²³hla.</td>
          <td>"A man is sitting."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᏧᏬᏝ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya ju¹wo⁴hla </td>
          <td>"the man who is sitting"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(9)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᎠᏯ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
          <td>"A man is speaking."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᎠᏯ ᏥᎦᏬᏂᎭ <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya jị²gạ²wo³ni⁴ha</td>
          <td>"the man who is speaking" </td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>function: j- marks relative clauses; it corresponds to the
      English relative pronouns "who," "which," and "that," as (10-11)
      indicate.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(10) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya gạ²wo³nị²ha.</td>
          <td>"A man is speaking."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᏥᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya jị²gạ²wo³ni⁴ha</td>
          <td>"the man who is speaking"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(11)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏟ ᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>gi²hli ji²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"I see a dog."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏟ ᏥᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ<br>
          </td>
          <td>gi²hli jị²ji²gowh¹tị⁴ha</td>
          <td>"the dog that I see"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: Since the relative prefix cannot be attached directly
      to a negated verb form, negative relatives are formed by adding
      the negative affixes n- (or ni- ) and -na to the verb form; the
      relative j- is then attached to the auxiliary verb -igi,yielding
      jigi, as in (12). When j- is prefixed, the second last syllable in
      the word takes pitch 4.</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 243</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(12) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ Ꮭ ᏱᎦᏬᏂᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya hla³ yị²gạ²wo³²nị²ha.</td>
          <td>"A man isn't speaking."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏍᎦᏯ ᏂᎦᏬᏂᏍᎬᎾ ᏥᎩ <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²sgạ²ya nị²gạ²wo³²ni²sgv⁴na ji⁴gi <br>
          </td>
          <td>"the man who isn't speaking"</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>Similarly, j- cannot occur in the same verb form with ga-
      "since," but it is possible for j- to be attached to -igi in
      instances in which the main verb is marked with ga-, as in (13).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(13) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎪᏯᎩ ᎬᏩᎪᎲᎢ ᏥᎩ <br>
          </td>
          <td>go¹hi³gi gv²wạ²go²hv⁴ʔi ji⁴gi</td>
          <td>"the one he hasn't seen in a long time"</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p><u>j-, specific past time</u></p>
    <p>form: j- before vowels, ji- before consonants, cf. (14-15); ju-
      before w.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(14) </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u¹wo³ni²sv²³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He spoke."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏒᎯ ᏧᏬᏂᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>sv²hi ju¹wo³ni²sv²³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He spoke yesterday."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(15)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᏬᏂᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>jạ²wo³ni²sv²³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"You spoke."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏒᎯ ᏥᏣᏬᏂᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>sv²hi jị²jạ²wo³ni²sv²³ʔi. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"You spoke yesterday."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>function: j- is used in sentences such as (14b and 13b) which
      contain a reference to a definite time in the past. It is also
      prefixed to verb forms in sentences which do not contain an
      explicit time reference if such a reference to a definite time in
      the past has previously been made or is understood by the speaker
      and hearer to exist; see (17), which would be the correct reply to
      (16).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(16) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏛᏁᎴᏒᎢ? <br>
          </td>
          <td>gạ²do² u¹dv³ne²le³sv²ʔi? <br>
          </td>
          <td>"What did he do yesterday?"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(17)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏧᏬᏂᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ju¹wo³ni²sv²³ʔi. </td>
          <td>"He spoke."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>The use of j- is required in sentences in which reference to a
      specific past time is understood, as noted above, provided that
      the past tense suffix -vʔi is employed. This suffix indicates that
      the speaker witnessed the action or state indicated by the verb
      form in question; when the reportative past tense suffix -eʔi is
      employed, j- cannot be used regardless of presence or absence of a
      specific time reference; cf. (18-19).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(18) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎤᏛᏁᎴᎢ ᏒᎢ? <br>
          </td>
          <td>gạ²do² u¹dv³ne²le³ʔi sv²hi? <br>
          </td>
          <td>"What did he do yesterday?"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(19)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏬᏂᏎᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u¹wo³ni²se³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He spoke(reportedly)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p><u>j-, negative imperative</u></p>
    <p>form: j- before vowels, ji- before consonants, cf. (20-21); ju-
      before w-.</p>
    <p>function: j- must be employed to mark negative commands; cf.
      (220-21)</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(20) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎢᏅᏂᎦ. <br>
          </td>

          <td>"Let's you and I hit him."</td>

          <td>"Let's you and I not hit him."</td>
        </tr>

          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏣᏚᎦ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>dị²jạ²du³²ga.</td>
          <td>"Throw them."</td>

          </td>
          <td>hle¹sdi jị²do²³jạ³de³si. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"Don't throw them."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: See Section <a href="#I-C">I-C</a> for a discussion of
      the suffixes employed in imperative forms; see the discussion of
      de- in this section for the forms which this prefix takes when it
      occurs together with j- as well as with other prefixes.</p>
    <p><u>j-, "again"- imperative</u></p>

      stop is inserted between it and the vowel which follows.</p>

      without is neutralized in the case of negative commands; see (24).</p>

    <p>function: n- is used optionally to indicate that the person
      spoken of is in a lateral position with respect to the speaker; in
      (36b), (37b), and (38b), the subject of the verb is understood to
      be standing with his side to the speaker.</p>
    <p><u>n- "already"</u></p>
    <p>form: n- before vowels, ni- before consonants, metathesis occurs
      when n- is followed by h-; cf. (36-38). Note that all the n-
      prefixes have the same forms exhibited by n-, lateral position,
      and illustrated by (36-38).</p>
    <p>function: n- may be attached to a past tense verb form to
      indicate that the action or state expressed by the verb had
      already taken place or was already in progress prior to some
      subsequent point in time; see (39).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(39) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎦᎿᏅᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u¹ga²hnạ²nv²³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"It rained."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏄᎦᎿᏃᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nu²ga³hnạ²no⁴ʔi. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"It had already rained."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>Sentences like (39b) can stand alone, provided that the necessary
      time reference has been established; typically, however, forms
      with n- form a clause in a sentence such as (40).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(40) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏄᎦᎿᏃᎢ ᏩᎩᎷᏣ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nu²ga³hnạ²no⁴ʔi wạ³gịʔ²luh²ja.</td>
          <td>"It had already rained when I arrived there."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: The use of n-, "already," requires the use of the
      habitual suffix -oʔi.</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 246</p>
    <p>Note that the verb in the independent clause may itself be in the
      habitual. When this is the case, the verb marked with n- is also
      understood to be habitual; note (41).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(41) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏄᏬᏂᏐᎢ ᏫᏥᎷᎪᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nu²wo³²ni²so⁴ʔi wị²jịʔ³luh²go³ʔi. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I arrive (habitually) there after he has already spoken."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>n- may also be used with the future progressive, as in (42).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(42) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎦᏬᏂᏍᎨᏍᏗ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nị²gạ²wo³²ni²sge⁴sdi. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He will already be speaking (at some specified future
            time)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p><u>n- ,negative</u></p>
    <p>form: cf. (36-38).</p>
    <p>function: n- is used together with the suffix -na to bring about
      negation in certain types of constructions; see (12) for an
      instance of this type of negation in relative clauses. Some
      additional examples follow in (43-44).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(43) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏲᏏᏍᎬᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u¹yo³si²sgv²³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He was hungry."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏄᏲᏏᏍᎬᎾ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nu²yo³²si²sgv⁴na.</td>
          <td>"He's without hunger."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(44)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᏍᎬᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>a¹gowt²ti²³sgv³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He was seeing it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᎪᏩᏘᏍᎬᎾ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nạ²gowh³ti²sgv⁴na. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He's without seeing it."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p><u>n-, specified action</u></p>
    <p>form: cf. (36-38).</p>
    <p>function: This prefix occurs with the verb a¹dv³nẹ²ha "to do"; it
      must be used with this verb when the activity being done has been
      specified or is understood; cf. (45-46).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(45) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᏙ ᎠᏛᏁᎭ? <br>
          </td>
          <td>gạ²do² a¹dv³nẹ²haʔ</td>
          <td>"What's he doing?"</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(46)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎪ ᎾᏛᏁᎭ? <br>
          </td>
          <td>ga³go<sup>2 </sup>na¹dv³nẹ²haʔ <br>
          </td>
          <td>"Who's doing it?"</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>In (46), the action referred to by the verb "to do" is understood
      to be known; in (45), on the other hand, it is understood that
      someone or something is doing something, but the exact nature of
      the action being performed is unknown. n- must be used in
      instances like (46) but may not be used in sentences like (45).</p>
    <p><u>n- , a prefix occurring before da-, motion toward speaker or
        position facing speaker</u></p>
    <p>form: This prefix is found only before da-, and therefore appears
      as ni-.</p>
    <p>function: There is an n- prefix which occurs in infinitive forms
      before da-, motion toward speaker, and before the da- form of di-
      position facing speaker; see (47).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(47) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᏓᏳᎪᏩᏛᏗ ᎤᏚᎵᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nị²dạ²yu³gowh²tvh³di u¹du²lị³ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He wants to see it (looking this way)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: This n- prefix does not seem to add any meaning to the
      verb forms in which it appears; it is simply inserted before the
      da- prefix in infinitive forms.</p>
    <p>It should be noted that a few verbs must occur with n-, ni- ; see
      (48).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(48) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏪᏌ ᏂᎦᏪᎠ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>we²³sa nị²gạ²wẹ²ʔa. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"A cat is meowing."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>In instances such as (48), the presence of n- does not seem to be
      meaningful, and the presence of n- in all forms of verbs like
      "meow" seems to be an arbitrary</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 247</p>
    <p>property of the verbs in question.</p>
    <p><u>de- plural object</u></p>

      - ; cf. (49-50).</p>

    <p>de- becomes do- before da-, future, before da-, motion toward
      speaker, and before di-, position facing speaker; cf. (62-64).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(62) <br>

          </td>

          <td>"I will see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>

          <td>b.</td>

          <td>"I will see them."</td>

    <p>Note that the sentence in (63b) is understood to refer to
      vehicles, not people; a different prefix would be needed in (63b)
      for an animate subject.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(64) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>

          <td>"He sees it (facing toward speaker)."</td>

    </table>
    <p>function: The primary function of de- is to indicate plurality of
      inanimate direct objects of transitive verbs; compare (65) with
      (66).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(65) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ ᎦᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²ni²sgạ²ya gạ²ji²gowh¹tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"I see men."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(66)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᎦᏁᏌᎢ ᏕᏥᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>dị²gạ²ne¹sạ³ʔi de²jị³gowh¹tị³ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I see boxes."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>de- may also indicate plurality of the action or state referred
      to by the verb; see (67).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(67) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᎠᏯ ᎦᏥᏯᎸᎢᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²ni²sgạ²ya gạ²ji²yạʔ²lv³ʔị²ha.</td>
          <td>"I'm tying up the men." (a single act of tying)</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᏂᏍᎦᏯ ᏕᎦᏥᏯᎸᎢᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ạ²ni²sgạʔ²ya de²gạ³ji²yạʔ²lv³ʔị²ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I'm tying up the men." (plural acts of tying)</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>In the case of (67a), it is understood that only one act of tying
      is taking place. (67b), however, indicates that two or more acts
      of tying are taking place. If six men were being tied, for
      example, (67b) would be appropriate to describe a situation in
      which each of the men were being tied individually, as well as a
      situation in which three men were tied together in two separate
      groups. (67b) could also be used to refer to a situation in which
      one group of men were being tied together, but with several ropes
      employed; the essential difference between (67a) and (67b) is,
      accordingly, one of plurality of the act described by the verb.</p>
    <p>Ambiguity can result from the use of de-; compare (68) with (69).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(68) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎦᎸᎢᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>gạʔ²lv³ʔị²ha.</td>
          <td>"I'm tying it up."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎦᎸᎢᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>de²gạʔ³lv<sup>3ʔ</sup>ị²ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I'm tying it up." (plural acts)</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(69)</td>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᎦᎸᎢᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>de²gạʔ³lv³ʔị²ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I'm tying them (in) up." (single or plural acts)</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>Forms (68b) and (69) are ambiguous unless the context clearly
      indicates to the hearer that the meaning of (68b) is intended;
      however, the form in question would ordinarily be understood to
      have the meaning of (69).</p>
    <p>Note that de- cannot be used to make the distinction between
      single and plural acts of the verb in instances such as (69),
      since its use is required in these cases to signal the plurality
      of the direct object of the verb.</p>
    <p>de- must be used with certain intransitive verbs in the plural.
      The verbs "to</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 249</p>
    <p>sit," "to stand," "to be hungry," and "to be thirsty," for
      example, require de- when the subject of the verb is plural, see
      (70).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(70) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᏲᏏᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u¹yo³sị²ha.</td>
          <td>"He's hungry."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏚᏂᏲᏏᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>du¹ni²yo³sị²ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"They are hungry."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>The presence of de- in forms like (70b) is redundant, since the
      plurality of the subject is indicated by the presence of the
      pronoun prefix -ni-; nevertheless, the failure to use de- in
      plural forms of intransitive verbs of this type results in
      ungrammatical sentences such as (71).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(71)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;"><span style="color: red;">*****</span><br>
          </td>
          <td><font color="red">*u¹ni²yo³sị²ha.</font></td>
          <td>"They are hungry."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>When sentences such as (71) are suggested to Cherokee speakers,
      they tend to react with amusement and they typically comment that
      (71) for example, seems to refer to two or more persons with one
      stomach. On the other hand, the use of de- with the singular form
      "he is hungry," as in (72), would seem to mean that a single
      individual has two or more stomachs.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(72)</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;"><span style="color: red;">*****</span><br>
          </td>
          <td><font color="red">*du¹yo³sị²ha.</font></td>
          <td>"He is hungry."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>In addition, other intransitive verbs require the use of de- with
      both singular and plural forms ; see (73).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(73) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏓᎩᏯᏪᎦ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>da¹gi²yạ²we³ga. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"I am tired."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏚᏂᏯᏪᎦ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>du¹ni²yạ²we³ga. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"They are tired."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>de- is apparently used with the singular form of the verb "to be
      tired" because the sensation of tiredness is perceived
      simultaneously in various parts of the body.</p>
    <p>comments: The use of de- with transitive verbs is illustrated in
      Section <a href="#I-B">I-B</a>, in Paradigm One.</p>

        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>

    <p>When da- is followed by -a-, the sequence of da- plus -a- yields
      dv-; see (76).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(76)&nbsp;<br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>a¹gowh²tị³ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He sees it. "</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏛᎪᎯ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>dv²go³hi.</td>
          <td>"He will see it."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>When da- is followed by -i -,the -i- is deleted; see (77).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>

    <p>When followed by the prefix -i - , "again," to be described
      below, da- plus -i- becomes dv-; see (78).</p>

    <p>da- plus a following -h- becomes t-; see (79).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(79) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎯᏬᏂᎭ. <br>

    <p>da- becomes di- before consonants and j- before vowels in certain
      construction which will be commented on below.</p>
    <p>function: da- is prefixed to a verb form to indicate that the
      action of the verb will take place in the future; cf. (77-79).
      Note that da- occurs together with the future tense suffix -i in
      these instances (see <a href="#I-C">I-C</a> for a discussion of
      the future suffix). When the -i suffix is added, the verb stem
      must appear in its nonprogressive form; see <a href="#I-C">I-C</a>
      for a discussion of the changes which occur in verb stems under
      suffixation.</p>
    <p>da- is used with the future suffix followed by the past tense
      suffix -vʔi to indicate that the subject of the verb was planning
      to perform the action of the verb in the past; see (80). Note that
      the future suffix becomes -is- before a vowel.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(80) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏓᎦᏬᏂᏏᏒᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>dạ²gạ²wo³²ni²si³sv²³ʔi. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He was going to speak."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>da- is used with the future suffix followed by the habitual
      suffix -oʔi to indicate that the subject of the verb is accustomed
      to speaking whenever the opportunity presents; see (81).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(81) <br>

    <p>When da- is used with the habitual, as in (81), it changes in
      form to di-, provided that a consonant follows; if a vowel
      follows, it changes to j-, as in (82).</p>

    <p>da- is used with the suffix -esdi to indicate that the subject of
      the verb will intend to perform the action expressed by the verb
      at a future time, as in (83). When da- is used in this
      construction, it appears as di- before consonants and j- before
      vowels.</p>

    <p>da- is used with the -i suffix followed by the reported past
      suffix -eʔi to indicate that the subject of the verb reportedly
      intended to speak in the past; see (84). In this construction
      also, da- appears as di- before consonants and as j- before
      vowels.</p>

    <p>The absence of the b-form in (87) is due to the fact that da-,
      future, cannot occur with di- distant position. In order to form
      the future tense in the case of a verb form with di- (or with da-,
      motion toward speaker), the suffix ordinarily used to form the
      progressive future must be used. As a result, forms like (87c) can
      be understood to have either the future progressive meaning or the
      nonprogressive one.</p>

    <p>The verb "to walk" is exceptional with regard to the form of da-
      expected with the habitual, reportative, and future progressive
      suffixes. Instead of da- appearing as di- before a following -a-
      as in (93b), (95b), and (97b) above, it appears as j- in the
      relevant forms of "to walk"; see (98).</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(98) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎠᎢᏎᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>a³ʔi²se³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He was reportedly walking."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏣᎢᏎᎢ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>ja³ʔi²se³ʔi.</td>
          <td>"He was reportedly walking (in the direction of the
            speaker)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>The exceptional status of "to walk" with respect to the forms of
      da- seems to be due to the presence of a pitch of level three on
      the -a- of "to walk."</p>
    <p>A sequence of da- plus -h- yields t-. This is the change
      described above for da-, future tense; cf. (79). Also, -i- is
      deleted after da-; see (77).</p>
    <p>function: da- is used with intransitive verbs of motion such as
      "walk," "run," and "crawl" to indicate that the direction of the
      motion is toward the position occupied by the speaker.</p>
    <p>comments: Transitive motion verbs such as "bring" seem to require
      the di- prefix, which indicates position facing speaker or motion
      toward speaker, as described below. In addition, some intransitive
      motion verbs take di- rather than da-. da- is thus extremely
      limited with respect to the number of verbs with which it can
      appear.</p>
    <p>It would be expected that da- would take the form j- before
      vowels other than -a- and -i-, just as the future tense prefix da-
      does; see (82). Since no intransitive motion verbs have been noted
      which begin with vowels other than -a-, no instances of da-
      appearing as j- have been found.</p>
    <p>Since da- is used to indicate that the subject of the verb is
      moving toward the speaker, the use of first person forms with da-
      is relatively rare; these forms are natural, however, in the case
      of non-present tense forms such as "I will be walking to the
      position which I am now occupying)." It is also possible to use
      da- in the present tense with first person forms in situations in
      which the speaker is watching himself in a movie, and commenting
      upon his own actions. In such situations it is possible for a
      speaker to say "I'm walking this way now" or something similar.</p>
    <p><u>di-, position facing speaker or motion toward speaker</u></p>
    <p>form: di- before consonants, j- before vowels other than -a - or
      -i -; cf. (99-100).</p>

    <p>When di- appears in a verb form ending in the past tense suffix
      -vʔi, it changes to da- before consonants and vowels, with a -y-
      inserted before vowels other than -a-; a following -a- is deleted;
      cf. (103-105). These changes occur in both progressive and
      non-progressive past tense forms with -vʔi, but not with -eʔi; see
      (100).</p>
    <table>

    <p>The changes noted above in (103-105) also take place in
      infinitive forms; in addition, any infinitive form with di- must
      be preceded by ni-. Note (107), in which di- changes to da- with
      -y- inserted before a vowel, and in which ni- is added to the
      infinitive form.</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(107) <br>
          </td>
          <td>a. <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎤᎪᏩᏛᏗ ᎤᏚᎵ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>u²gowh²tvh³di u¹du²li.</td>
          <td>"He wants him to see it."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏂᏓᏳᎪᏩᏛᏗ ᎤᏚᎵ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>nị²dạ²yu³gowh²tvh³di u¹du²li.</td>
          <td>"He wants him to see it (facing the speaker)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>While ni- is also inserted before da-, motion toward speaker, in
      infinitive forms, the forms of da- remain the same; i.e., the
      forms of da- which occur following ni- in infinitives are those
      illustrated by (88-91).</p>
    <p>function: di- is employed with non-motion verbs to indicate that
      the subject of the verb is facing the speaker. It also occurs with
      verbs such as the "to fight," which involve motion which does not
      take place in a straight line; i.e., although there is obviously
      motion involved in fighting, the motion is essentially
      non-directional. Transitive motion verbs such as "to bring" also
      take di-; in forms in like (108), it is understood that the
      subject of the verb is bringing the object in question to a
      position adjacent to the speaker. With a few verbs, such as "to
      fight," di- simply indicates that the subject of the verb is in a
      distant view from the speaker. This function of di- seems to be
      limited to verbs such as "to fight" in which the nature of the
      action indicated by the verb involves non-directional motion.</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 254</p>
    <table>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td>(108) <br>
          </td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏗᏲᎯᎭ. <br>
          </td>
          <td>di²hyo³hị²ha. <br>
          </td>
          <td>"He is bringing it (to a position adjacent to the
            speaker)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>comments: As indicated in Figure Two, da-, future, da- motion
      toward speaker, and di- are mutually exclusive. Further study may
      indicate that da-, motion toward speaker, and di- position facing
      speaker or motion toward speaker, should be analyzed as a single
      prefix. Such an analysis would require fairly detailed rules to
      provide the correct form of the prefix in the various contexts
      described above.</p>

    <p>A peculiarity of the i- prefix is its behavior in forms with a
      vowel following. In these cases, instead of a glottal stop being
      inserted, the prefix may appear simply as high pitch on the vowel
      which would otherwise have directly followed i- ; cf. (115- 116) .</p>

    <p>The option of deleting the prefix with a change in pitch taking
      place on the following vowel is a peculiarity of i-, and
      apparently has to do with the fact that i- carries high pitch,
      i.e. pitch three.</p>

        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᎡᏍᏗᎪᏩᏔ <br>
          </td>
          <td>e²sdi²³go³hwah²ta.</td>
          <td>"You (dl) see it (facing the speaker)."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td>(129)</td>
          <td>a.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏕᏍᏗᎪᏩᏘᎭ <br>
          </td>
          <td>de²sdi³gowh²tị³ha.</td>
          <td>"You (dl) see them."</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td><br>
          </td>
          <td>b.</td>
          <td style="vertical-align: top;">ᏙᏗᏍᏗᎪᏩᏔ <br>
          </td>
          <td>do²dị²sdi³go²hwah³ta.</td>
          <td>"You (dl) see them (facing the speaker)."</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p>function: e- is used with imperative forms when the person spoken
      to is facing the speaker from a position some distance away.</p>
    <p>comments: An alternative to considering e- and di-, position
      facing speaker, to be two distinct prefixes is to analyze them as
      a single prefix di-, with a variant form e- which occurs in
      imperative forms when not preceded by another prefix.</p>
    <p>I-B.<a name="I-B"></a> <u>The pronoun prefixes</u>. The purpose
      of this section is to describe the pronoun prefixes which directly
      precede verb stems in Cherokee. A number of verb paradigms,
      illustrating these prefixes, will be presented and commented on.</p>
    <p>Verb stems in Cherokee must be preceded by one or more pronoun
      prefixes which refer to the subject of the verb. In the case of
      transitive verbs, the presence of a direct or indirect object is
      also indicated by the pronoun prefixes. There are two sets of
      pronoun prefixes in Cherokee, Set A and Set B; while most verbs
      require the use of the Set A prefixes, a large number of Cherokee
      verbs also occur with prefixes from Set B. In the paradigms which
      follow, both sets of prefixes will be illustrated in paradigms and
      commented on.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm One: "to tie up"</u></p>
    <p>The verb forms in this paradigm exhibit Set A pronoun prefixes as
      they appear before a transitive verb stem beginning with a vowel .
      The stem of the verb "to tie up" is either -aʔlvʔih- or -ahlvʔih-,
      depending on the particular combination of subject and object; see
      the <u>Notes on Paradigm One</u> below. This paradigm is thus
      illustrative of the alternation between glottal stop and -h- which
      characterizes a number of Cherokee verbs. In addition, Paradigm
      One has been chosen to illustrate the use of the plural prefix
      de-; otherwise identical forms are presented which contrast with
      respect to the presence or absence of de- demonstrating the
      function of this prefix throughout the paradigm.</p>

    <br>
    <p align="center">Notes on Paradigm One</p>
    <p><u>The analysis of the pronoun prefixes</u>. It is frequently the
      case in Cherokee, as well as in many other American Indian
      languages, that combinations of subject and object prefixes cannot
      readily be analyzed into their component parts. In the above
      paradigm, the meaning of each subject-object pronoun combination
      is given, followed by an analysis of the individual elements found
      within that combination. In a number of cases alternative analyses
      are possible.</p>
    <p><u>Person, number, and gender</u>. The pronoun prefixes indicate
      whether verbal subjects and objects are first, second, or third
      person. In addition, non-singular first person forms must be
      either inclusive or exclusive. First person dual inclusive forms
      are translated into English with "you and I"; first person plural
      inclusive forms are translated "you (pl) and I." Dual and plural
      first person exclusive forms are translated "he and I" and "they
      and I" respectively.</p>
    <p>There is a contrast between singular and plural in all persons in
      Cherokee. In addition, there is a dual number which appears in
      first and second person forms but not in the third person.</p>
    <p>The category of gender is present in the third person, where
      there is a contrast between animate and inanimate objects of
      verbs. It should be noted that Cherokee has no sex gender.</p>
    <p><u>The alternation between -h- and glottal stop</u>. The stem of
      the verb "to tie up" exhibits the alternation between -h- and
      glottal stop which is characteristic of many Cherokee verbs.
      Glottal stop appears under the following conditions: in all forms
      with a first person singular subject; in forms with a second
      person singular subject and a third person animate or first person
      exclusive object; in forms with</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 265</p>
    <p>a first person inclusive subject and a third person animate
      object; in forms with a first person exclusive subject and a third
      person animate or second person dual or plural object. In all
      other cases, -h- appears.</p>
    <p><u>The use of the plural prefix de-</u>. The use of the plural
      prefix de- to indicate both plurality of the action or state of
      the verb and also to mark third person inanimate objects of verbs
      is illustrated in Paradigm One. See Section <a href="#I-A">I-A</a>
      for a more complete description of the functions of this prefix.</p>
    <p><u>The initial plural marker i -</u>. A redundant plural marker,
      i- , appears in forms 22, 92, 97, and 103. This initial element
      may or may not be present; many Cherokee speakers apparently never
      use verb forms with redundant i- .</p>
    <p><u>A process of linguistic change</u>. Many Cherokee speakers
      make no distinction between third person plural animate and
      inanimate direct objects in forms with first or second person dual
      or plural subjects. For these speakers, the third person plural
      animate object marker has been replaced by the plural prefix de-
      (or one of its variant forms) which is used to mark third person
      plural inanimate direct objects. For example, the form ᎨᏍᏓᎸᎢᎭ
      ge²sdạʔ²lv³ʔị²ha "you (dl) are tying them (an) up" has been
      replaced, in some dialects of Cherokee, by the form ᏕᏍᏓᏢᎢᎭ
      de²³sdạ³hlv³ʔị²ha. The latter form has thus come to mean "you (dl)
      are tying them an or in) up" in these dialects.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Two : "to hit</u>"</p>
    <p>This paradigm illustrates the use of the Set A prefixes with a
      verb stem beginning with a vowel, as in Paradigm One. The stem of
      "hit" exhibits an alternation between -h- and its absence; -h-
      appears under the same conditions as in Paradigm One , but note
      that there are no instances of glottal stop.</p>

    <p><u>The use of the plural prefix de- with forms of "to hit.</u>"
      Since the use of the plural prefix de- was illustrated in Paradigm
      One, forms in which de- indicates plurality of the action of the
      verb are not given in this paradigm nor in subsequent paradigms.
      It should be pointed out, however, that forms such as ᎨᏨᏂᎭ
      ge²jv²nị³ha "you (pl) are hitting them" (see 57) are relatively
      uncommon. Since it is difficult to conceive of a situation in
      which three or more individuals are collectively hitting a second
      group of individuals, the form ᏕᎨᏨᏂᎭ de²ge³jv²nị³ha is much more
      common than is ᎨᏨᏂᎭ&nbsp; ge²jv²nị³ha. In ᏕᎨᏨᏂᎭ&nbsp;
      de²ge³jv²nị³ha, it is understood that there are a number of
      individual acts of hitting. Forms like ᎨᏨᏂᎭ&nbsp; ge²jv²nị³ha do
      occur, however, and would be used, for example, if a car
      containing several occupants were to strike a second car with
      several occupants.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Three: "to see"</u></p>
    <p>The stem of the verb "to see" is -gowhtih- (or -gowahtih-). This
      verb requires the use of the Set A prefixes, as do the verbs "to
      tie up"and "to hit." An important</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 268</p>
    <p>difference exists, however: since the stem of "to see" begins
      with a consonant, the prefixes appear in somewhat different form.
      In particular, the insertion of -y- between the final vowel of the
      prefix and the initial vowel of the stem does not take place.
      Other differences will be noted below.</p>

          <td style="width: 326px;">"they see you (dl)" </td>
          <td style="width: 454px;">cf. P1, 122; -i- is inserted.</td>
        </tr>

          <td>(67) <br>
          </td>

          </td>

          <td style="width: 326px;">"they see them (an or in)" </td>

        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>
    <p><br>
    </p>
    <p align="center">Notes on Paradigm Three</p>
    <p><u>The insertion of -y- and of -i- between the pronoun prefix and
        the verb stem</u>. A -y- is inserted between pronoun prefixes
      ending in a vowel and a following verb stem beginning with a
      vowel; numerous examples may be seen in Paradigms One and Two. On
      the other hand, an -i- is inserted between a pronoun prefix ending
      in a consonant and a verb stem beginning with a consonant; see the
      forms in this paradigm for examples.</p>
    <p><u>The first person singular subject pronoun prefix.</u> The
      first person singular subject pronoun prefix occurs as g- before
      stems beginning with a vowel; before stems beginning with a
      consonant, -i- is inserted and g- changes to j-.</p>
    <p><u>The third person singular subject pronoun prefix.</u> The form
      of the third person singular subject pronoun prefix is g- before
      verb stems beginning with a vowel, and either ga- or a- before
      stems beginning with a consonant. A few verbs with stems beginning
      with vowels do not take a subject pronoun prefix in the third
      person singular.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Four: "to forget"</u></p>
    <p>A number of Cherokee verbs require a second set of pronoun
      prefixes, which we will refer to as the Set B prefixes. While most
      of these verbs are intransitive, some transitive verbs do take the
      Set B prefixes. One such verb is -vkewsg- "to forget." Most of the
      subject-object pronoun prefix combinations found in paradigms of
      verbs taking the Set B prefixes are the same as those used with
      verbs requiring the Set A prefixes. The following paradigm is
      therefore a partial one, with only those forms listed which
      exhibit pronoun prefixes which differ from the corresponding Set A
      prefixes. The correct pronoun prefix for any form not given below
      can be determined by finding the form in Paradigm One. For
      example, the form for "I am forgetting you," ᎬᏴᎨᏩᏍᎦ gv²yv¹kew²sga,
      is not below since the prefix gv- is found in the form for "I am
      tying you up" ᎬᏯᎸᎢᎭ gv²yạʔ²lv³ʔị²ha in Paradigm One. In both
      cases, since the verb stem begins with a vowel, -y- is inserted.</p>

    <p align="center">Notes on Paradigm Four</p>
    <p><u>The contrast between third person animate and inanimate direct
        objects in forms with third person subjects.</u> In the paradigm
      of "forget" there is a contrast between third person animate and
      third person inanimate direct objects in verb forms with third
      person subjects. Compare, for example, ᎥᎨᏩᏍᎦ&nbsp; gv²kew²sga
      "he's for- getting him" and ᎤᏩᎨᏩᏍᎦ u¹wa²kew²sga "he's forgetting
      it." This contrast is not present in paradigms of verbs requiring
      the Set A prefixes, nor in the paradigms of all transitive verbs
      which take the Set B prefixes.</p>
    <p><u>The change from -v to -a in third person forms.</u> In verbs
      which take the Set B prefixes, stem initial -v changes to -a in
      third person forms; note the forms ᎪᏋᎨᏩᏍᎦ a¹gwv²kew²sga "I'm
      forgetting it" and ᎤᏩᎨᏩᏍᎦ u¹wa²kew²sga "he's forgetting it."</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Five: "to like" </u></p>
    <p>The paradigm of "to like," like the paradigm of "to forget,"
      further illustrates the use of the Set B prefixes with transitive
      verbs. There are some important differences between the paradigms
      of "to like" and "to forget," however. The stem</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 274</p>
    <p>of "to like," -lvkwd-, begins with a consonant rather than a
      vowel. In addition, the contrast between third person animate and
      third person inanimate direct objects in forms with third person
      subjects does not appear in this paradigm. As in the paradigm of
      "to forget," only those forms which differ from the corresponding
      prefix forms in Paradigms One, Two, and Three are listed below. To
      find the correct prefix for any form of "to like" not given below,
      see the corresponding form in Paradigm Three. Since the stem of
      "like" begins with a consonant, no -y- is inserted between
      prefixes ending in a vowel and the stem. An -i- is inserted,
      however, between prefixes ending in a consonant and the initial
      consonant of the stem.</p>

        <tr>

    <p align="center">Notes on Paradigm Six</p>
    <p> <u>Intransitive verb stems beginning with vowels which require
        the Set A prefixes.</u> The Set A prefixes which appear in this
      paradigm are also used with</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 276</p>
    <p>intransitive stems beginning with a vowel, with the following
      differences. The form of the first person singular prefix is g-,
      and the third person singular is g- or is not marked, depending on
      the verb in question. In addition, -i- is never inserted as it is
      in the case of stems beginning with a consonant.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Seven: "to be hungry"</u></p>
    <p>The stem of "to be hungry" requires the Set B prefixes. The stem
      is -yosih-, requiring the insertion of -i- after a prefix ending
      in a consonant.</p>

    <p align="center">Notes on Paradigm Seven</p>
    <p><u> Intransitive verb stems beginning with vowels which require
        the Set B prefixes.</u> The Set B prefixes used with the forms
      of "to be hungry" also occur with intransitive stems beginning
      with a vowel, with the following changes. The first singular
      prefix</p>
    <hr color="darkgray">
    <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 277</p>
    <p>changes from agi- to agw-, the third person singular prefix is
      uw-, and -i- is never inserted as it is in the case of stems
      beginning with a consonant.</p>
    <p><u>The use of the plural prefix de- with the verb "to be hungry."</u>
      The plural prefix de- must be used with the non-singular forms of
      "to be hungry." Failure to use de- in plural forms of this verb
      leads to ludicrous results. For example, the form <font
        color="red">*u¹ni²yo³sị²ha</font> "to be hungry" would seem to
      imply to Cherokee speakers that two or more individuals have a
      single stomach. Other verbs which behave similarly are "they are
      thirsty" and "they are tired." "To be tired" requires the plural
      prefix in singular form, presumably because the sensation of
      tiredness is felt in all parts of the body.</p>
    <p><u>Paradigm Eight: "to hit," past tense</u></p>
    <p>The stems of all Cherokee verbs must appear in one of two basic
      forms: progressive or non-progressive. In the preceding paradigms,
      each verb appeared with the progressive form of the stem. In
      Section <a href="#I-C">I-C</a>, the difference between
      progressive and non-progressive stems will be discussed further.
      For our present purposes it is sufficient to point out that the
      Set A prefixes are used only with progressive verb stems. Those
      verbs which require the Set A prefixes with progressive stems take
      Set B prefixes with non-progressive stems. There are, accordingly,
      two classes of verbs in Cherokee: those which appear with the Set
      B prefixes regardless of the form of the stem, and those which
      take Set A prefixes with progressive stems and Set B prefixes with
      non-progressive stems. "Hit" is an instance of the latter group of
      verbs. In the following paradigm the stem of "hit" is in its
      non-progressive form, -hnil-, followed by the past tense suffix
      -vʔi. Only those forms are listed which exhibit subject-object
      prefix combinations differing from the corresponding prefixes in
      Paradigm Two. To determine the correct prefix for any form not
      given, find the corresponding form in Paradigm Two.</p>
    <table>

    <p align="center"><u>Notes on Paradigm Eight</u></p>
    <p><u>The alternation between -h- and the absence of -h-.</u> The
      alternation between -h- and its absence is characteristic of of
      both the progressive and non-progressive stem of "hit." It is
      apparently the case in Cherokee that alternations of this kind, as
      well as alternations between -h- and glottal stop, always occur in
      both the progressive and non-progressive stems of the verb subject
      to them.</p>
    <p><u>The Change from Set A to Set B prefixes in verbs with stems
        beginning with a consonant.</u> To determine the correct form of
      a Set B prefix with a non-progressive stem of a verb like "see,"
      find the corresponding prefix in Paradigm Five. For example, the
      prefix used in the form ᎠᎩᎸᏆᏗ a¹gị²lv²³kwdi "I like it" is also used in
      the form ᎠᎩᎪᎲᎢ a¹gị²go²hv²³ʔi "I saw it."</p>
    <summary of="" section="" <a="" href="#I-B">I-B
      <p> The various sets of pronoun prefixes illustrated in the
        preceding paradigms are displayed below in figures 1--4. Figure
        1 is a display of the Set A subject pronoun prefixes which occur
        with intransitive verbs. The Set B subject pronoun prefixes are
        shown in Figure 2. Figure 3 display subject-object pronoun
        combinations which occur with transitive verb stems taking the
        Set A prefixes. Figure 4 shows those Set B subject-object prefix
        combinations differ from the corresponding Set A prefixes in
        Figure 3.</p>
      <p><u>The Set A subject pronoun prefixes</u>. The Set A subject
        pronoun prefixes which occur with intransitive verbs are
        displayed in Figure 1. The numerals 1, 2, and 3 indicate whether
        a give prefix is first, second, or third person. The headings
        singular, dual, and plural indicate the number of each prefix.
        Note that the label "inclusive" is employed to refer to forms in
        which the speaker and the person or persons spoken to are the
        subject of the verb; e.g. ᎢᏂᏬᏂᎭ i¹ni²wo³nị²ha "you and I are speaking"
        is a first person dual inclusive form. The label "exclusive" is
        used to refer to forms in which the person spoken to is excluded
        while the speaker and one or more other persons are included as
        the subject; e.g. ᎣᏍᏗᏬᏂᎭ o¹sdi²wo³nị²ha "he and I are speaking" is a
        first person dual exclusive form. It should be noted that the
        pronoun prefixes displayed in Figures 1--4 are presented as
        wholes; i.e., they are not further analyzed. For such analysis,
        see the analysis presented above with each paradigm. Each form
        is presented first as it appears before a stem beginning with a
        vowel; directly below this form the same prefix is shown as it
        appears before a stem beginning with a consonant. For example,
        the form of the first person singular subject pronoun prefix
        before a stem beginning with a vowel is g-; the form of the same
        prefix before a consonant is ji-, however. Note the third person
        singular prefix forms. Some verb stems beginning with vowels
        require the prefix g-, while others take no prefix in third
        person singular; e.g. ᎬᏃᏌᏍᎦ gv²no²sạ³sga "he is sweeping" but ᎡᎦ e³ga "he
        is going." Some verb stems beginning with consonants take ga- in
        the third person singular, while others require a-; e.g.
        ᎦᏬᏂᎭ gạ²wo³nị²ha "he is speaking," but ᎠᎪᏩᏘᎭ a¹gowh²tị³ha "he sees it."</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">

      <p><u>The Set A subject-object prefixes.</u> The complex
        subject-object prefixes used with transitive verb stems
        requiring the Set A prefixes are shown in Figure 3. To find a
        given subject-object prefix, find the label for the subject
        component of the prefix at the left of Figure 3 and the label
        for the object component at the top of the chart. The box where
        the row labeled or the subject component and the column labeled
        for the object component intersect will contain the prefix. For
        example, to find the prefix used in verb forms with first person
        singular subjects and third person singular animate objects,
        locate the intersection of the row labeled first person singular
        subject and the column labeled third person singular animate
        object. The box located at that point will contain the prefix
        being sought.</p>

      <p><u>The reflexive prefix -adad-.</u> The reflexive prefix -adad-
        occurs following the pronoun prefixes and directly preceding the
        verb stern. This prefix is described in Section <a href="#I-E">I-E</a>.</p>
      <p>I-C.<a name="I-C"></a> <u>The non-final suffixes.</u> Every
        verb form in Cherokee must end in a suffix. Those suffixes which
        can end verb forms are referred to as final suffixes. Most final
        suffixes, however, are not limited to occurrence in final
        position: two or more of these suffixes frequently occur
        together in a single verb form. The final suffixes will be
        discussed in Section <a href="#I-D">I-D</a>.</p>
      <p>There are also a number of suffixes which cannot end verb
        forms. One or more of these non-final suffixes may occur in a
        verb form, followed by at least one final suffix. The non-final
        suffixes are discussed individually in this section. Figure 1
        shows the relative order in which the non-final and final
        suffixes occur following the verb stern. Final suffixes are
        indicated by an (f) following the suffix label. The suffixes in
        box 13 are limited to final position.</p>
      <hr color="darkgray">
      <p style="color: rgb(128, 128, 128);">Page 282</p>
